href="/wiki/Book:Thermodynamics" title="Book:Thermodynamics">\n          Book:Thermodynamics\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">\n         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">\n          <ul>\n           <li class="nv-view">\n            <a href="/wiki/Template:Thermodynamics_sidebar" title="Template:Thermodynamics sidebar">\n             <abbr title="View this template">\n              v\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n           <li class="nv-talk">\n            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Thermodynamics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Thermodynamics sidebar">\n             <abbr title="Discuss this template">\n              t\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n           <li class="nv-edit">\n            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Thermodynamics_sidebar&amp;action=edit">\n             <abbr title="Edit this template">\n              e\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n          </ul>\n         </div>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        Enthalpy\n       </b>\n       <span class="nowrap">\n        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">\n         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">\n          /\n          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">\n           <span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\n            \xcb\x88\n           </span>\n           <span title="/\xc9\x9b/: \'e\' in \'dress\'">\n            \xc9\x9b\n           </span>\n           <span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">\n            n\n           </span>\n           <span title="/\xce\xb8/: \'th\' in \'thigh\'">\n            \xce\xb8\n           </span>\n           <span title="/\xc9\x99l/: \'le\' in \'bottle\'">\n            \xc9\x99l\n           </span>\n           <span title="\'p\' in \'pie\'">\n            p\n           </span>\n           <span title="/i\xcb\x90/ or /\xc9\xaa/: \'y\' in \'happy\'">\n            i\n           </span>\n          </span>\n          /\n         </a>\n        </span>\n        <small class="nowrap">\n         (\n         <span class="unicode haudio">\n          <span class="fn">\n           <span style="white-space:nowrap">\n            <a href="/wiki/File:En-us-enthalpy.ogg" title="About this sound">\n             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>\n            </a>\n           </span>\n           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1c/En-us-enthalpy.ogg" title="En-us-enthalpy.ogg">\n            listen\n           </a>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n         )\n        </small>\n       </span>\n       is a measurement of\n       <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">\n        energy\n       </a>\n       in a\n       <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system" title="Thermodynamic system">\n        thermodynamic system\n       </a>\n       . It is equal to the internal energy of the system plus the product of pressure and volume.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-1">\n         [1]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       More technically, it includes the\n       <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">\n        internal energy\n       </a>\n       , which is the energy required to create a system, and the amount of energy required to make room for it by displacing its\n       <a href="/wiki/Environment_(systems)" title="Environment (systems)">\n        environment\n       </a>\n       and establishing its volume and pressure.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-2">\n         [2]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Enthalpy is defined as a\n       <a href="/wiki/State_function" title="State function">\n        state function\n       </a>\n       that depends only on the prevailing equilibrium state identified by the system\'s internal energy, pressure, and volume. It is an\n       <a href="/wiki/Intensive_and_extensive_properties" title="Intensive and extensive properties">\n        extensive\n       </a>\n       quantity. The unit of measurement for enthalpy in the\n       <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">\n        International System of Units\n       </a>\n       (SI) is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">\n        joule\n       </a>\n       , but other historical, conventional units are still in use, such as the\n       <a href="/wiki/British_thermal_unit" title="British thermal unit">\n        British thermal unit\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">\n        calorie\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Enthalpy is the preferred expression of system energy changes in many chemical, biological, and physical measurements at constant pressure, because it simplifies the description of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_transfer" title="Energy transfer">\n        energy transfer\n       </a>\n       . At constant pressure, the enthalpy change equals the energy transferred from the environment through heating or work other than expansion work.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The total enthalpy,\n       <i>\n        H\n       </i>\n       , of a system cannot be measured directly. The same situation exists in classical mechanics: only a change or difference in energy carries physical meaning. Enthalpy itself is a thermodynamic potential, so in order to measure the enthalpy of a system, we must refer to a defined reference point; therefore what we measure is the change in enthalpy, \xce\x94\n       <i>\n        H\n       </i>\n       . The \xce\x94\n       <i>\n        H\n       </i>\n       is a positive change in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">\n        endothermic\n       </a>\n       reactions, and negative in heat-releasing\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">\n        exothermic\n       </a>\n       processes.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       For processes under constant pressure, \xce\x94\n       <i>\n        H\n       </i>\n       is equal to the change in the internal energy of the system, plus the\n       <a href="/wiki/Work_(thermodynamics)#Pressure-volume_work" title="Work (thermodynamics)">\n        pressure-volume work\n       </a>\n       that the system has done on its surroundings.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-3">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This means that the change in enthalpy under such conditions is the heat absorbed (or released) by the material through a chemical reaction or by external heat transfer. Enthalpies for chemical substances at constant pressure assume\n       <a href="/wiki/Standard_state" title="Standard state">\n        standard state\n       </a>\n       : most commonly 1 bar pressure. Standard state does not, strictly speaking, specify a temperature (see\n       <a href="/wiki/Standard_state" title="Standard state">\n        standard state\n       </a>\n       ), but expressions for enthalpy generally reference the standard heat of formation at 25\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0C.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Enthalpy of\n       <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">\n        ideal gases\n       </a>\n       and incompressible solids and liquids does not depend on pressure, unlike\n       <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">\n        entropy\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Gibbs_free_energy" title="Gibbs free energy">\n        Gibbs energy\n       </a>\n       . Real materials at common temperatures and pressures usually closely approximate this behavior, which greatly simplifies enthalpy calculation and use in practical designs and analyses.\n      </p>\n
